	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i></h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		<div id="contentSub2"></div>
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the role-playing game. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons (disambiguation)">Dungeons &amp; Dragons (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"D&amp;D" and "DnD" redirect here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/D%26D_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="D&amp;D (disambiguation)">D&amp;D (disambiguation)</a> and <a href="/wiki/DND_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="DND (disambiguation)">DND (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Fantasy role-playing game</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><i><b>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</b></i> (commonly abbreviated as <i><b>D&amp;D</b></i> or <i><b>DnD</b></i>)<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a> <a href="/wiki/Tabletop_role-playing_game" title="Tabletop role-playing game">tabletop role-playing game</a> (RPG) originally designed by <a href="/wiki/Gary_Gygax" title="Gary Gygax">Gary Gygax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dave_Arneson" title="Dave Arneson">Dave Arneson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> It was first published in 1974 by <a href="/wiki/TSR_(company)#Tactical_Studies_Rules" class="mw-redirect" title="TSR (company)">Tactical Studies Rules, Inc.</a> (TSR).<sup id="cite_ref-:2_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The game has been published by <a href="/wiki/Wizards_of_the_Coast" title="Wizards of the Coast">Wizards of the Coast</a> (now a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Hasbro" title="Hasbro">Hasbro</a>) since 1997. It was derived from <a href="/wiki/Miniature_wargaming" title="Miniature wargaming">miniature wargames</a>, with a variation of the 1971 game <a href="/wiki/Chainmail_(game)" title="Chainmail (game)"><i>Chainmail</i></a> serving as the initial rule system.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Banshee_Gygax_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banshee_Gygax-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <i>D&amp;D</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> publication is commonly recognized as the beginning of modern role-playing games and the role-playing game industry.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>D&amp;D</i> departs from traditional <a href="/wiki/Wargame" title="Wargame">wargaming</a> by allowing each player to create their own <a href="/wiki/Player_character" title="Player character">character</a> to play instead of a <a href="/wiki/Military_formation" class="mw-redirect" title="Military formation">military formation</a>. These characters embark upon imaginary adventures within a fantasy setting. A <a href="/wiki/Dungeon_Master" title="Dungeon Master">Dungeon Master</a> (DM) serves as the game's referee and storyteller, while maintaining the setting in which the adventures occur, and playing the role of the inhabitants of the game world. The characters form a party and they interact with the setting's inhabitants and each other. Together they solve dilemmas, engage in battles, explore, and gather treasure and knowledge. In the process, the characters earn <a href="/wiki/Experience_point" title="Experience point">experience points</a> (XP) in order to rise in levels, and become increasingly powerful over a series of separate gaming sessions.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The early success of <i>D&amp;D</i> led to a proliferation of similar game systems. Despite the competition, <i>D&amp;D</i> has remained as the market leader in the role-playing game industry.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In 1977, the game was split into two branches: the relatively rules-light game system of <a href="/wiki/Editions_of_Dungeons_%26_Dragons#Dungeons_&amp;_Dragons_Basic_Set_and_revisions" title="Editions of Dungeons &amp; Dragons">basic <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i></a>, and the more structured, rules-heavy game system of <i><a href="/wiki/Editions_of_Dungeons_%26_Dragons#Advanced_Dungeons_&amp;_Dragons" title="Editions of Dungeons &amp; Dragons">Advanced Dungeons &amp; Dragons</a></i> (abbreviated as <i>AD&amp;D</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Dragon_26_Gygax_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dragon_26_Gygax-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <i>AD&amp;D</i> 2nd Edition was published in 1989. In 2000, a new system was released as <i>D&amp;D</i> 3rd edition, continuing the edition numbering from <i>AD&amp;D</i>; a revised version 3.5 was released in June 2003. These 3rd edition rules formed the basis of the <a href="/wiki/D20_system" class="mw-redirect" title="D20 system">d20 System</a>, which is available under the <a href="/wiki/Open_Game_License" title="Open Game License">Open Game License</a> (OGL) for use by other publishers. <i>D&amp;D</i> 4th edition was released in June 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The 5th edition of <i>D&amp;D</i>, the most recent, was released during the second half of 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2004, <i>D&amp;D</i> remained the best-known,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and best-selling,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> role-playing game in the US, with an estimated 20 million people having played the game, and more than US$1 billion in book and equipment sales worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The year 2017 had "the most number of players in its history—12 million to 15 million in North America alone".<sup id="cite_ref-Brodeur_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brodeur-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> <i>D&amp;D 5th edition</i> sales "were up 41 percent in 2017 from the year before, and soared another 52 percent in 2018, the game's biggest sales year yet".<sup id="cite_ref-:4_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The game has been supplemented by many pre-made <a href="/wiki/Adventure_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)" title="Adventure (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">adventures</a>, as well as commercial <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_campaign_settings" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons campaign settings">campaign settings</a> suitable for use by regular gaming groups.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <i>D&amp;D</i> is known beyond the game itself for other <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons-related_products" class="mw-redirect" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons-related products"><i>D&amp;D</i>-branded products</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_in_popular_culture" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons in popular culture">references in popular culture</a>, and some of the <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_controversies" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons controversies">controversies</a> that have surrounded it, particularly a <a href="/wiki/Moral_panic" title="Moral panic">moral panic</a> in the 1980s falsely linking it to <a href="/wiki/Satanism" title="Satanism">Satanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moral_Panic_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moral_Panic-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The game has won multiple awards and has been translated into many languages.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Development_history"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Development history</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Sources_and_influences"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Sources and influences</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Edition_history"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Edition history</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Play_overview">Play overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Play overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:D%26D_Game_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/D%26D_Game_1.jpg/250px-D%26D_Game_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/D%26D_Game_1.jpg/375px-D%26D_Game_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/D%26D_Game_1.jpg/500px-D%26D_Game_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:D%26D_Game_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An elaborate <i>D&amp;D</i> game in progress. Among the gaming aids here are dice, a variety of miniatures and a dungeon <a href="/wiki/Diorama" title="Diorama">diorama</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> is a structured yet open-ended role-playing game. It is normally played indoors with the participants seated around a tabletop. Typically, each player controls only a single character, which represents an individual in a fictional setting.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> When working together as a group, these <a href="/wiki/Player_characters" class="mw-redirect" title="Player characters">player characters</a> (PCs) are often described as a "party" of adventurers, with each member often having their own area of specialty which contributes to the success of the whole.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> During the course of play, each player directs the actions of their character and their interactions with other characters in the game.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> This activity is performed through the verbal impersonation of the characters by the players, while employing a variety of social and other useful cognitive skills, such as logic, basic mathematics and imagination.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> A game often continues over a series of meetings to complete a single <a href="/wiki/Adventure_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)" title="Adventure (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">adventure</a>, and longer into a series of related gaming adventures, called a "<a href="/wiki/Campaign_(role-playing_games)" title="Campaign (role-playing games)">campaign</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-:3_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:8_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The results of the party's choices and the overall storyline for the game are determined by the <a href="/wiki/Dungeon_Master" title="Dungeon Master">Dungeon Master</a> (DM) according to the rules of the game and the DM's interpretation of those rules.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:9_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The DM selects and describes the various <a href="/wiki/Non-player_character" title="Non-player character">non-player characters</a> (NPCs) that the party encounters, the settings in which these interactions occur, and the outcomes of those encounters based on the players' choices and actions.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:7_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Encounters often take the form of battles with "<a href="/wiki/Monsters_in_Dungeons_%26_Dragons" title="Monsters in Dungeons &amp; Dragons">monsters</a>"&#160;– a generic term used in <i>D&amp;D</i> to describe potentially hostile beings such as animals, aberrant beings, or mythical creatures.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The game's extensive rules&#160;– which cover diverse subjects such as social interactions,<sup id="cite_ref-:9_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Magic_of_Dungeons_%26_Dragons" class="mw-redirect" title="Magic of Dungeons &amp; Dragons">magic use</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> combat,<sup id="cite_ref-:9_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and the effect of the environment on PCs<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>&#160;– help the DM to make these decisions. The DM may choose to deviate from the published rules<sup id="cite_ref-:9_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> or make up new ones if they feel it is necessary.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most recent versions of the game's rules are detailed in three <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_manuals" class="mw-redirect" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons manuals">core rulebooks</a>: The <i><a href="/wiki/Player%27s_Handbook" title="Player&#39;s Handbook">Player's Handbook</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Dungeon_Master%27s_Guide" title="Dungeon Master&#39;s Guide">Dungeon Master's Guide</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Monster_Manual" title="Monster Manual">Monster Manual</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The only items required to play the game are the rulebooks, a <a href="/wiki/Character_sheet" title="Character sheet">character sheet</a> for each player, and a number of <a href="/wiki/Polyhedral_dice" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyhedral dice">polyhedral dice</a>. Many players also use miniature figures on a grid map as a visual aid, particularly during combat. Some editions of the game presume such usage. Many optional accessories are available to enhance the game, such as expansion rulebooks, pre-designed adventures and various <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_campaign_settings" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons campaign settings">campaign settings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:7_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Game_mechanics">Game mechanics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Game mechanics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_gameplay" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons gameplay">Dungeons &amp; Dragons gameplay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Character_class_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)" title="Character class (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">Character class (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dice_(typical_role_playing_game_dice).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Dice_%28typical_role_playing_game_dice%29.jpg/250px-Dice_%28typical_role_playing_game_dice%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Dice_%28typical_role_playing_game_dice%29.jpg/375px-Dice_%28typical_role_playing_game_dice%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Dice_%28typical_role_playing_game_dice%29.jpg/500px-Dice_%28typical_role_playing_game_dice%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3070" data-file-height="2314" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dice_(typical_role_playing_game_dice).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>D&amp;D</i> uses polyhedral dice to resolve in-game events. These are abbreviated by a 'd' followed by the number of sides. Shown counter-clockwise from the bottom are: d4, d6, d8, d10, d12 and d20 dice. A pair of d10 can be used together to represent percentile dice, or d100.</div></div></div>
<p>Before the game begins, each player <a href="/wiki/Character_creation" title="Character creation">creates</a> their player character and records the details (described below) on a character sheet. First, a player determines their character's <a href="/wiki/Game_mechanics_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)#Ability_scores" class="mw-redirect" title="Game mechanics (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">ability scores</a>, which consist of Strength, Constitution, Dexterity, Intelligence, Wisdom, and Charisma. Each edition of the game has offered differing methods of determining these statistics.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The player then chooses a race (species) such as human or elf, a <a href="/wiki/Character_class_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)" title="Character class (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">character class</a> (occupation) such as fighter or wizard, an <a href="/wiki/Alignment_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)" title="Alignment (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">alignment</a> (a moral and ethical outlook), and other features to round out the character's abilities and backstory, which have varied in nature through differing editions.
</p><p>During the game, players describe their PCs' intended actions, such as punching an opponent or picking a lock, and converse with the DM, who then describes the result or response.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Trivial actions, such as picking up a letter or opening an unlocked door, are usually automatically successful. The outcomes of more complex or risky actions are determined by rolling dice.<sup id="cite_ref-Culture_chapter_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Culture_chapter-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Different polyhedral dice are used for different actions, such as a twenty-sided die to see whether a hit was made in combat, but an eight-sided die to determine how much damage was dealt.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Factors contributing to the outcome include the character's ability scores, skills and the difficulty of the task.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In circumstances where a character does not have control of an event, such as when a trap or magical effect is triggered or a spell is cast, a <a href="/wiki/Saving_throw" title="Saving throw">saving throw</a> can be used to determine whether the resulting damage is reduced or avoided.<sup id="cite_ref-PHB_p.136_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PHB_p.136-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In this case the odds of success are influenced by the character's class, levels and ability scores.<sup id="cite_ref-PHB_p.136_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PHB_p.136-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the game is played, each PC changes over time and generally increases in capability. Characters gain (or sometimes lose) experience, skills<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and wealth, and may even alter their alignment<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> or gain <a href="/wiki/Character_class_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)#Multi-classing" title="Character class (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">additional character classes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The key way characters progress is by earning <a href="/wiki/Experience_point" title="Experience point">experience points</a> (XP), which happens when they defeat an enemy or accomplish a difficult task.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Acquiring enough XP allows a PC to advance a <a href="/wiki/Experience_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Experience level">level</a>, which grants the character improved class features, abilities and skills.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> XP can be lost in some circumstances, such as encounters with creatures that drain life energy, or by use of certain magical powers that come with an XP cost.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hit_point" class="mw-redirect" title="Hit point">Hit points</a> (HP) are a measure of a character's vitality and health and are determined by the class, level and constitution of each character. They can be temporarily lost when a character sustains wounds in combat or otherwise comes to harm, and loss of HP is the most common way for a character to die in the game.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Death can also result from the loss of key ability scores<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> or character levels.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> When a PC dies, it is often possible for the dead character to be resurrected through magic, although some penalties may be imposed as a result. If resurrection is not possible or not desired, the player may instead create a new PC to resume playing the game.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adventures_and_campaigns">Adventures and campaigns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Adventures and campaigns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Adventure_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)" title="Adventure (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">Adventure (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_campaign_settings" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons campaign settings">Dungeons &amp; Dragons campaign settings</a></div>
<p>A typical <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> game consists of an "adventure", which is roughly equivalent to a single story.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The DM can either design an original adventure, or follow one of the many pre-made adventures (also known as "modules") that have been published throughout the history of <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i>. Published adventures typically include a background story, illustrations, maps and goals for PCs to achieve. Some include location descriptions and handouts. Although a small adventure entitled "<a href="/wiki/Temple_of_the_Frog" title="Temple of the Frog">Temple of the Frog</a>" was included in the <i><a href="/wiki/Blackmoor_(supplement)" title="Blackmoor (supplement)">Blackmoor</a></i> rules supplement in 1975, the first stand-alone <i>D&amp;D</i> module published by TSR was 1978's <i><a href="/wiki/Steading_of_the_Hill_Giant_Chief" class="mw-redirect" title="Steading of the Hill Giant Chief">Steading of the Hill Giant Chief</a></i>, written by Gygax.
</p><p>A linked series of adventures is commonly referred to as a "<a href="/wiki/Campaign_(role-playing_games)" title="Campaign (role-playing games)">campaign</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The locations where these adventures occur, such as a city, country, planet or an entire <a href="/wiki/Fictional_universe" title="Fictional universe">fictional universe</a>, are referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Campaign_setting" title="Campaign setting">campaign settings</a>" or "world".<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> <i>D&amp;D</i> settings are based in various fantasy genres and feature different levels and types of magic and technology.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Popular commercially published <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_campaign_settings" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons campaign settings">campaign settings for <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i></a> include <a href="/wiki/Greyhawk" title="Greyhawk">Greyhawk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dragonlance" title="Dragonlance">Dragonlance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forgotten_Realms" title="Forgotten Realms">Forgotten Realms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mystara" title="Mystara">Mystara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spelljammer" title="Spelljammer">Spelljammer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ravenloft" title="Ravenloft">Ravenloft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dark_Sun" title="Dark Sun">Dark Sun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planescape" title="Planescape">Planescape</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birthright_(campaign_setting)" title="Birthright (campaign setting)">Birthright</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eberron" title="Eberron">Eberron</a>.
</p><p>In addition to first-party campaigns and modules, two campaigns based on popular culture have been created. The first, based on <i><a href="/wiki/Stranger_Things" title="Stranger Things">Stranger Things</a></i>, was released in May 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> A campaign based on the <a href="/wiki/Rick_and_Morty#Comics" title="Rick and Morty"><i>Rick and Morty vs. Dungeons and Dragons</i></a> comic book series was later released in November 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Alternatively, DMs may develop their own fictional worlds to use as campaign settings.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Miniature_figures">Miniature figures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Miniature figures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Miniature_figure_(gaming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Miniature figure (gaming)">Miniature figure (gaming)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Miniatures_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a1/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Miniatures_2.jpg/300px-Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Miniatures_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/a1/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Miniatures_2.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="422" data-file-height="236" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Miniatures_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> miniature figures. The grid mat underneath uses one-inch squares.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wargaming" class="mw-redirect" title="Wargaming">wargames</a> from which <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> evolved used miniature figures to represent combatants. <i>D&amp;D</i> initially continued the use of miniatures in a fashion similar to its direct precursors. The original <i>D&amp;D</i> set of 1974 required the use of the <i><a href="/wiki/Chainmail_(game)" title="Chainmail (game)">Chainmail</a></i> miniatures game for combat resolution.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> By the publication of the 1977 game editions, combat was mostly resolved verbally. Thus miniatures were no longer required for game play, although some players continued to use them as a visual reference.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1970s, numerous companies began to sell miniature figures specifically for <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> and similar games. Licensed miniature manufacturers who produced official figures include <a href="/wiki/Grenadier_Miniatures" class="mw-redirect" title="Grenadier Miniatures">Grenadier Miniatures</a> (1980–1983),<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Citadel_Miniatures" title="Citadel Miniatures">Citadel Miniatures</a> (1984–1986),<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ral_Partha" class="mw-redirect" title="Ral Partha">Ral Partha</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and TSR itself.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Most of these miniatures used the 25&#160;mm scale.
</p><p>Periodically, <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> has returned to its wargaming roots with supplementary rules systems for miniatures-based wargaming. Supplements such as <i><a href="/wiki/Battlesystem" title="Battlesystem">Battlesystem</a></i> (1985 and 1989) and a new edition of <i>Chainmail</i> (2001)<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> provided rule systems to handle battles between armies by using miniatures.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_history">Development history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Development history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources_and_influences">Sources and influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sources and influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sources_and_influences_on_the_development_of_Dungeons_%26_Dragons" title="Sources and influences on the development of Dungeons &amp; Dragons">Sources and influences on the development of Dungeons &amp; Dragons</a></div>
<p>An immediate predecessor of <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> was a set of medieval miniature rules written by <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Perren" title="Jeff Perren">Jeff Perren</a>. These were expanded by <a href="/wiki/Gary_Gygax" title="Gary Gygax">Gary Gygax</a>, whose additions included a fantasy supplement, before the game was published as <i><a href="/wiki/Chainmail_(game)" title="Chainmail (game)">Chainmail</a></i>. When <a href="/wiki/Dave_Wesely" class="mw-redirect" title="Dave Wesely">Dave Wesely</a> entered the Army in 1970, his friend and fellow Napoleonics wargamer <a href="/wiki/Dave_Arneson" title="Dave Arneson">Dave Arneson</a> began a medieval variation of Wesely's <a href="/wiki/Braunstein_(wargame)" class="mw-redirect" title="Braunstein (wargame)">Braunstein</a> games, where players control individuals instead of armies.<sup id="cite_ref-DW3_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DW3-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Arneson used <i>Chainmail</i> to resolve combat.<sup id="cite_ref-Banshee_Gygax_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banshee_Gygax-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> As play progressed, Arneson added such innovations as character classes, experience points, level advancement, armor class, and others.<sup id="cite_ref-DW3_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DW3-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Having partnered previously with Gygax on <i><a href="/wiki/Don%27t_Give_Up_the_Ship!" class="mw-redirect" title="Don&#39;t Give Up the Ship!">Don't Give Up the Ship!</a></i>, Arneson introduced Gygax to his Blackmoor game and the two then collaborated on developing "The Fantasy Game", the game that became <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i>, with the final writing and preparation of the text being done by Gygax.<sup id="cite_ref-D&amp;Dfaq_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-D&amp;Dfaq-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wired_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wired-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-history_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The name was chosen by Gygax's two-year-old daughter Cindy; upon being presented with a number of choices of possible names, she exclaimed, "Oh Daddy, I like Dungeons &amp; Dragons best!", although less prevalent versions of the story gave credit to his wife Mary Jo.<sup id="cite_ref-witwer_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-witwer-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>101</span></sup>
</p><p>Many <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> elements appear in hobbies of the mid-to-late 20th century. For example, character-based role playing can be seen in <a href="/wiki/Improvisational_theater" class="mw-redirect" title="Improvisational theater">improvisational theater</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Game-world simulations were well developed in wargaming. Fantasy milieux specifically designed for gaming could be seen in <a href="/wiki/Glorantha" title="Glorantha">Glorantha</a>'s board games among others.<sup id="cite_ref-Schick_17-34_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schick_17-34-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately, however, <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> represents a unique blending of these elements.
</p><p>The world of <i>D&amp;D</i> was influenced by world mythology, history, <a href="/wiki/Pulp_magazine" title="Pulp magazine">pulp fiction</a>, and contemporary fantasy novels. The importance of <a href="/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien" title="J. R. R. Tolkien">J. R. R. Tolkien</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings" title="The Lord of the Rings">The Lord of the Rings</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hobbit" title="The Hobbit">The Hobbit</a></i> as an influence on <i>D&amp;D</i> is controversial. The presence in the game of <a href="/wiki/Halfling_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)" class="mw-redirect" title="Halfling (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">halflings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elf_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)" title="Elf (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">elves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Half-elf_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)" title="Half-elf (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">half-elves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)" title="Dwarf (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">dwarves</a>, orcs, <a href="/wiki/Ranger_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)" title="Ranger (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">rangers</a>, and the like, draw comparisons to these works. The resemblance was even closer before the threat of copyright action from <a href="/wiki/Middle-earth_Enterprises" title="Middle-earth Enterprises">Tolkien Enterprises</a> prompted the name changes of <a href="/wiki/Hobbit" title="Hobbit">hobbit</a> to 'halfling', <a href="/wiki/Ent" title="Ent">ent</a> to '<a href="/wiki/Treant" class="mw-redirect" title="Treant">treant</a>', and <a href="/wiki/Balrog" title="Balrog">balrog</a> to '<a href="/wiki/Balor_(Dungeons_%26_Dragons)" class="mw-redirect" title="Balor (Dungeons &amp; Dragons)">balor</a>'. For many years, Gygax played down the influence of Tolkien on the development of the game.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dragon95_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dragon95-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> However, in an interview in 2000, he acknowledged that Tolkien's work had a "strong impact" though he also said that the list of other influential authors was long.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>D&amp;D</i> magic system, in which wizards memorize spells that are used up once cast and must be re-memorized the next day, was heavily influenced by the <i><a href="/wiki/Dying_Earth_series" class="mw-redirect" title="Dying Earth series">Dying Earth</a></i> stories and novels of <a href="/wiki/Jack_Vance" title="Jack Vance">Jack Vance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The original alignment system (which grouped all characters and creatures into 'Law', 'Neutrality' and 'Chaos') was derived from the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Hearts_and_Three_Lions" title="Three Hearts and Three Lions">Three Hearts and Three Lions</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Poul_Anderson" title="Poul Anderson">Poul Anderson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lit_source_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lit_source-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> A troll described in this work influenced the <i>D&amp;D</i> definition of that monster.<sup id="cite_ref-Dragon95_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dragon95-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other influences include the works of <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Howard" title="Robert E. Howard">Robert E. Howard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Rice_Burroughs" title="Edgar Rice Burroughs">Edgar Rice Burroughs</a>, <a href="/wiki/A._Merritt" title="A. Merritt">A. Merritt</a>, <a href="/wiki/H._P._Lovecraft" title="H. P. Lovecraft">H. P. Lovecraft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Leiber" title="Fritz Leiber">Fritz Leiber</a>, <a href="/wiki/L._Sprague_de_Camp" title="L. Sprague de Camp">L. Sprague de Camp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fletcher_Pratt" title="Fletcher Pratt">Fletcher Pratt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Zelazny" title="Roger Zelazny">Roger Zelazny</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moorcock" title="Michael Moorcock">Michael Moorcock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Monsters, spells, and magic items used in the game have been inspired by hundreds of individual works such as <a href="/wiki/Van_vogt" class="mw-redirect" title="Van vogt">A. E. van Vogt's</a> "Black Destroyer", <a href="/wiki/Coeurl" title="Coeurl">Coeurl</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Displacer_Beast" class="mw-redirect" title="Displacer Beast">Displacer Beast</a>), <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Carroll" title="Lewis Carroll">Lewis Carroll</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Jabberwocky" title="Jabberwocky">Jabberwocky</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Vorpal" class="mw-redirect" title="Vorpal">vorpal</a> sword) and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Book of Genesis</a> (the clerical spell 'Blade Barrier' was inspired by the "<a href="/wiki/Flaming_sword_(mythology)" title="Flaming sword (mythology)">flaming sword</a> which turned every way" at the gates of <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden" title="Garden of Eden">Eden</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-lit_source_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lit_source-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Edition_history">Edition history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Edition history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Editions_of_Dungeons_%26_Dragons" title="Editions of Dungeons &amp; Dragons">Editions of Dungeons &amp; Dragons</a></div>
<p><i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> has gone through several revisions. Parallel versions and inconsistent naming practices can make it difficult to distinguish between the different editions.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_game">Original game</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Original game">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_(1974)" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons (1974)">original <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i></a>, now referred to as <i>OD&amp;D</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> was a small box set of three booklets published in 1974. With a very limited production budget of only $2000 — with only $100 budgeted for artwork<sup id="cite_ref-aa_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aa-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>26</span></sup> — it was amateurish in production and assumed the player was familiar with wargaming. Nevertheless, it grew rapidly in popularity, first among wargamers and then expanding to a more general audience of college and high school students. Roughly 1,000 copies of the game were sold in the first year followed by 3,000 in 1975, and many more in the following years.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> This first set went through many printings and was supplemented with several official additions, such as the original <a href="/wiki/Greyhawk_(supplement)" title="Greyhawk (supplement)">Greyhawk</a> and Blackmoor supplements (both 1975),<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> as well as magazine articles in TSR's official publications and many <a href="/wiki/Fanzine" title="Fanzine">fanzines</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Two-pronged_strategy">Two-pronged strategy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Two-pronged strategy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In early 1977, TSR created the first element of a two-pronged strategy that would divide <i>D&amp;D</i> for nearly two decades. A <i><a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Basic_Set" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons Basic Set">Dungeons &amp; Dragons Basic Set</a></i> boxed edition was introduced that cleaned up the presentation of the essential rules, made the system understandable to the general public, and was sold in a package that could be stocked in toy stores.<sup id="cite_ref-history_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Later in 1977, the first part of <i>Advanced Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> (<i>AD&amp;D</i>) was published,<sup id="cite_ref-history_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> which brought together the various published rules, options and corrections, then expanded them into a definitive, unified game for hobbyist gamers. TSR marketed them as an introductory game for new players and a more complex game for experienced ones; the <i>Basic Set</i> directed players who exhausted the possibilities of that game to switch to the advanced rules.
</p><p>As a result of this parallel development, the basic game included many rules and concepts which contradicted comparable ones in <i>AD&amp;D</i>. <a href="/wiki/John_Eric_Holmes" title="John Eric Holmes">John Eric Holmes</a>, the editor of the basic game, preferred a lighter tone with more room for personal improvisation. <i>AD&amp;D</i>, on the other hand, was designed to create a tighter, more structured game system than the loose framework of the original game.<sup id="cite_ref-Dragon_26_Gygax_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dragon_26_Gygax-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Between 1977 and 1979, three hardcover rulebooks, commonly referred to as the "core rulebooks", were released: the <i><a href="/wiki/Player%27s_Handbook" title="Player&#39;s Handbook">Player's Handbook</a></i> (PHB), the <i><a href="/wiki/Dungeon_Master%27s_Guide" title="Dungeon Master&#39;s Guide">Dungeon Master's Guide</a></i> (DMG), and the <i><a href="/wiki/Monster_Manual" title="Monster Manual">Monster Manual</a></i> (MM). Several supplementary books were published throughout the 1980s, notably <i><a href="/wiki/Unearthed_Arcana" title="Unearthed Arcana">Unearthed Arcana</a></i> (1985) that included a large number of new rules.<sup id="cite_ref-history_67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Confusing matters further, the original <i>D&amp;D</i> boxed set remained in publication until 1979, since it remained a healthy seller for TSR.<sup id="cite_ref-Schick_17-34_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schick_17-34-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Revised_editions">Revised editions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Revised editions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the 1980s, the rules for <i>Advanced Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> and "basic" <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> remained separate, each developing along different paths.
</p><p>In 1981, the basic version of <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> was revised by <a href="/wiki/Tom_Moldvay" title="Tom Moldvay">Tom Moldvay</a> to make it even more novice-friendly. It was promoted as a continuation of the original <i>D&amp;D</i> tone, whereas <i>AD&amp;D</i> was promoted as advancement of the mechanics.<sup id="cite_ref-Dragon_26_Gygax_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dragon_26_Gygax-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> An accompanying <i><a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Expert_Set" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons Expert Set">Expert Set</a></i>, originally written by <a href="/wiki/David_Cook_(game_designer)" title="David Cook (game designer)">David "Zeb" Cook</a>, allowed players to continue using the simpler ruleset beyond the early levels of play. In 1983, revisions of those sets by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Mentzer" title="Frank Mentzer">Frank Mentzer</a> were released, revising the presentation of the rules to a more tutorial format. These were followed by <i><a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Companion_Set" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons Companion Set">Companion</a></i> (1983), <i><a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Master_Rules" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons Master Rules">Master</a></i> (1985), and <i><a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Immortals_Rules" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons Immortals Rules">Immortals</a></i> (1986) sets.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schick_133_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schick_133-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Each set covered game play for more powerful characters than the previous.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The first four sets were compiled in 1991 as a single hardcover book, the <i><a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Rules_Cyclopedia" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons Rules Cyclopedia">Dungeons &amp; Dragons Rules Cyclopedia</a></i>, which was released alongside a new <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Game_(1991_boxed_set)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons Game (1991 boxed set)">introductory boxed set</a>.
</p><p><i>Advanced Dungeons &amp; Dragons 2nd Edition</i> was published in 1989,<sup id="cite_ref-history_67-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> again as three core rulebooks; the primary designer was David "Zeb" Cook. The <i>Monster Manual</i> was replaced by the <i><a href="/wiki/Monstrous_Compendium" title="Monstrous Compendium">Monstrous Compendium</a></i>, a loose-leaf binder that was subsequently replaced by the hardcover <i><a href="/wiki/Monstrous_Manual" class="mw-redirect" title="Monstrous Manual">Monstrous Manual</a></i> in 1993. In 1995, the core rulebooks were slightly revised, although still referred to by TSR as the 2nd Edition,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> and a series of <i>Player's Option</i> manuals were released as optional rulebooks.<sup id="cite_ref-history_67-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The release of <i>AD&amp;D 2nd Edition</i> deliberately excluded some aspects of the game that had attracted negative publicity. References to demons and devils, sexually suggestive artwork, and playable, evil-aligned character types&#160;– such as assassins and half-orcs&#160;– were removed.<sup id="cite_ref-Dragon_154_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dragon_154-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The edition moved away from a theme of 1960s and 1970s "sword and sorcery" fantasy fiction to a mixture of medieval history and mythology.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The rules underwent minor changes, including the addition of non-weapon proficiencies&#160;– skill-like abilities that originally appeared in 1st Edition supplements. The game's magic spells were divided into schools and spheres.<sup id="cite_ref-D&amp;Dfaq_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-D&amp;Dfaq-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> A major difference was the promotion of various game settings beyond that of traditional fantasy. This included blending fantasy with other genres, such as horror (Ravenloft), science fiction (Spelljammer), and apocalyptic (Dark Sun), as well as alternative historical and non-European mythological settings.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wizards_of_the_Coast">Wizards of the Coast</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Wizards of the Coast">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1997, a near-bankrupt TSR was purchased by <a href="/wiki/Wizards_of_the_Coast" title="Wizards of the Coast">Wizards of the Coast</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-briefhistory_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-briefhistory-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Following three years of development, <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> 3rd edition was released in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The new release folded the Basic and Advanced lines back into a single unified game. It was the largest revision of the <i>D&amp;D</i> rules to date, and served as the basis for a multi-genre role-playing system designed around 20-sided dice, called the <a href="/wiki/D20_System" title="D20 System">d20 System</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The 3rd Edition rules were designed to be internally consistent and less restrictive than previous editions of the game, allowing players more flexibility to create the characters they wanted to play.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Skills and feats were introduced into the core rules to encourage further customization of characters.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The new rules standardized the mechanics of action resolution and combat.<sup id="cite_ref-PHB_p.4_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PHB_p.4-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In 2003, <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons v.3.5</i> was released as a revision of the 3rd Edition rules. This release incorporated hundreds of rule changes, mostly minor, and expanded the core rulebooks.<sup id="cite_ref-PHB_p.4_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PHB_p.4-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In early 2005, Wizards of the Coast's R&amp;D team started to develop <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons 4th Edition</i>, prompted mainly by the feedback obtained from the <i>D&amp;D</i> playing community and a desire to make the game faster, more intuitive, and with a better play experience than under the 3rd Edition. The new game was developed through a number of design phases spanning from May 2005 until its release.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons 4th Edition</i> was announced at <a href="/wiki/Gen_Con" title="Gen Con">Gen Con</a> in August 2007, and the initial three core books were released June 6, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-excitingnews_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-excitingnews-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> 4th Edition streamlined the game into a simplified form and introduced numerous rules changes. Many character abilities were restructured into "Powers". These altered the spell-using classes by adding abilities that could be used at will, per encounter, or per day. Likewise, non-magic-using classes were provided with parallel sets of options. Software tools, including player character and monster building programs, became a major part of the game.<sup id="cite_ref-svensson_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-svensson-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On January 9, 2012, Wizards of the Coast announced that it was working on a 5th edition of the game.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The company planned to take suggestions from players and let them <a href="/wiki/Playtest" title="Playtest">playtest</a> the rules.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Public playtesting began on May 24, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> At Gen Con 2012 in August, <a href="/wiki/Mike_Mearls" title="Mike Mearls">Mike Mearls</a>, lead developer for 5th Edition, said that Wizards of the Coast had received feedback from more than 75,000 playtesters, but that the entire development process would take two years, adding, "I can't emphasize this enough&#160;... we're very serious about taking the time we need to get this right."<sup id="cite_ref-forbes-gen-con-2012_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forbes-gen-con-2012-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The release of the 5th Edition, coinciding with <i>D&amp;D</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s 40th anniversary, occurred in the second half of 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the release of 5th edition, there have been over twenty <i>Dungeon &amp; Dragons</i> books published including new <a href="/wiki/List_of_Dungeons_%26_Dragons_rulebooks#Dungeons_&amp;_Dragons_5th_edition" title="List of Dungeons &amp; Dragons rulebooks">rulebooks, campaign guides and adventure modules</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> 2017 had "the most number of players in its history — 12 million to 15 million in North America alone".<sup id="cite_ref-Brodeur_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brodeur-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Mary Pilon, for <i><a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_Businessweek" title="Bloomberg Businessweek">Bloomberg</a></i>, reported that sales of 5th edition <i>Dungeon &amp; Dragons</i> "were up 41 percent in 2017 from the year before, and soared another 52 percent in 2018, the game’s biggest sales year yet. [...] In 2017, 9 million people watched others play D&amp;D on <a href="/wiki/Twitch_(service)" title="Twitch (service)">Twitch</a>, immersing themselves in the world of the game without ever having to pick up a die or cast a spell".<sup id="cite_ref-:72_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:72-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2020, Wizards of the Coast announced that Dungeons &amp; Dragons had its 6th annual year of growth in 2019 with a "300 percent increase in sales of their <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Starter_Set" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons Starter Set">introductory box sets</a>, as well as a 65% increase on sales in Europe, a rate which has more than quadrupled since 2014".<sup id="cite_ref-:10_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> In terms of player demographics in 2019, 39% of identified as female and 61% identified as male. 40% of players are considered <a href="/wiki/Generation_Z" title="Generation Z">Gen Z</a> (24 years old or younger), 34% of players are in the age range of 25-34 and 26% of players are aged 35+.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Licensing">Licensing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Licensing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early in the game's history, TSR took no action against small publishers' production of <i>D&amp;D</i> compatible material, and even licensed <a href="/wiki/Judges_Guild" title="Judges Guild">Judges Guild</a> to produce <i>D&amp;D</i> materials for several years, such as <i><a href="/wiki/City_State_of_the_Invincible_Overlord" title="City State of the Invincible Overlord">City State of the Invincible Overlord</a>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-Kyngdoms_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kyngdoms-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> This attitude changed in the mid-1980s when TSR took legal action to try to prevent others from publishing compatible material. This angered many fans and led to resentment by the other gaming companies.<sup id="cite_ref-Schick_17-34_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schick_17-34-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Although TSR took legal action against several publishers in an attempt to restrict third-party usage, it never brought any court cases to completion, instead settling out of court in every instance.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> TSR itself ran afoul of intellectual property law in several cases.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the launch of <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons'</i>s <a href="/wiki/Editions_of_Dungeons_%26_Dragons" title="Editions of Dungeons &amp; Dragons">3rd Edition</a>, Wizards of the Coast made the d20 System available under the <a href="/wiki/Open_Game_License" title="Open Game License">Open Game License</a> (OGL) and <a href="/wiki/D20_System#Trademark_license" title="D20 System">d20 System trademark license</a>. Under these licenses, authors were free to use the d20 System when writing games and game supplements.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The OGL and d20 Trademark License made possible new games, some based on licensed products like <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars_Roleplaying_Game_(Wizards_of_the_Coast)" title="Star Wars Roleplaying Game (Wizards of the Coast)">Star Wars</a></i>, and new versions of older games, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Call_of_Cthulhu_(role-playing_game)#D20_Call_of_Cthulhu" title="Call of Cthulhu (role-playing game)">Call of Cthulhu</a></i>.
</p><p>With the release of the <a href="/wiki/Editions_of_Dungeons_%26_Dragons#Dungeons_&amp;_Dragons_4th_edition" title="Editions of Dungeons &amp; Dragons">fourth edition</a>, Wizards of the Coast introduced its <a href="/wiki/Game_System_License" title="Game System License">Game System License</a>, which represented a significant restriction compared to the very open policies embodied by the OGL. In part as a response to this, some publishers (such as <a href="/wiki/Paizo_Publishing" class="mw-redirect" title="Paizo Publishing">Paizo Publishing</a> with its <i><a href="/wiki/Pathfinder_Roleplaying_Game" title="Pathfinder Roleplaying Game">Pathfinder Roleplaying Game</a></i>) who previously produced materials in support of the <i>D&amp;D</i> product line, decided to continue supporting the 3rd Edition rules, thereby competing directly with Wizards of the Coast. Others, such as <a href="/wiki/Kenzer_%26_Company" title="Kenzer &amp; Company">Kenzer &amp; Company</a>, are returning to the practice of publishing unlicensed supplements and arguing that copyright law does not allow Wizards of the Coast to restrict third-party usage.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 2000s, there has been a trend towards reviving and recreating older editions of <i>D&amp;D</i>, known as the <a href="/wiki/Old_School_Revival" title="Old School Revival">Old School Revival</a>. This in turn inspired the creation of <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_retro-clones" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons retro-clones">"retro-clones"</a>, games which more closely recreate the original rule sets, using material placed under the OGL along with non-copyrightable mechanical aspects of the older rules to create a new presentation of the games.
</p><p>Alongside the publication of the 5th Edition, Wizards of the Coast established a two-pronged licensing approach. The core of the 5th Edition rules have been made available under the OGL, while publishers and independent creators have also been given the opportunity to create licensed materials directly for Dungeons &amp; Dragons and associated properties like the Forgotten Realms under a program called the <a href="/wiki/OneBookShelf#Dungeon_Masters_Guild" title="OneBookShelf">DM's Guild</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The DM's Guild does not function under the OGL, but uses a community agreement intended to foster liberal cooperation among content creators.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wizards of the Coast has started to release 5th Edition products that tie into other intellectual properties — such as <i><a href="/wiki/Magic:_The_Gathering" title="Magic: The Gathering">Magic: The Gathering</a></i> with the <i>Guildmasters' Guide to Ravnica</i> (2018) and <i>Mythic Odysseys of Theros</i> (2020) source books.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Two 5th Edition <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons_Starter_Set#Dungeons_&amp;_Dragons_5th_Edition" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons Starter Set">starter box sets</a> based on TV shows, <i><a href="/wiki/Stranger_Things" title="Stranger Things">Stranger Things</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Rick_and_Morty" title="Rick and Morty">Rick and Morty</a></i>, were released in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Source books based on <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> live play series have also been released: <i>Acquisitions Incorporated</i> (2019) and <i><a href="/wiki/Explorer%27s_Guide_to_Wildemount" title="Explorer&#39;s Guide to Wildemount">Explorer's Guide to Wildemount</a></i> (2020).<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Eric_Goldberg_(game_designer)" title="Eric Goldberg (game designer)">Eric Goldberg</a> reviewed <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> in <i><a href="/wiki/Ares_(magazine)" title="Ares (magazine)">Ares Magazine</a></i> #1 (March 1980), rating it a 6 out of 9, and commented that "<i>Dungeons and Dragons</i> is an impressive achievement based on the concept alone, and also must be credited with cementing the marriage between the fantasy genre and gaming."<sup id="cite_ref-Ares1_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ares1-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eric_Goldberg_(game_designer)" title="Eric Goldberg (game designer)">Eric Goldberg</a> again reviewed <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> in <i><a href="/wiki/Ares_(magazine)" title="Ares (magazine)">Ares Magazine</a></i> #3 and commented that "<i>D&amp;D</i> is the FRP game played most often in most places."<sup id="cite_ref-Ares3_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ares3-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The game had more than three million players around the world by 1981,<sup id="cite_ref-inc_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inc-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> and copies of the rules were selling at a rate of about 750,000 per year by 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Beginning with a <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French language</a> edition in 1982, <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> has been translated into many languages beyond the original English.<sup id="cite_ref-D&amp;Dfaq_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-D&amp;Dfaq-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-history_67-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> By 2004, consumers had spent more than US$1&#160;billion on <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> products and the game had been played by more than 20&#160;million people.<sup id="cite_ref-waters_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-waters-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> As many as six million people played the game in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-svensson_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-svensson-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Later editions would lead to inevitable comparisons between the game series. <a href="/wiki/Griffin_McElroy" title="Griffin McElroy">Griffin McElroy</a>, for <i><a href="/wiki/Polygon_(website)" title="Polygon (website)">Polygon</a></i>, wrote: "The game has shifted in the past four decades, bouncing between different rules sets, philosophies and methods of play. Role-playing, character customization and real-life improvisational storytelling has always been at the game's core, but how those ideas are interpreted by the game system has changed drastically edition-to-edition".<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Dieter Bohn, for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Verge" title="The Verge">The Verge</a></i>, wrote: "Every few years there’s been a new version of <i>D&amp;D</i> that tries to address the shortcomings of the previous version and also make itself more palatable to its age. [...] The third edition got a reputation (which it didn’t necessarily deserve) for being too complex and rules-focused. The fourth edition got a reputation (which it didn’t necessarily deserve) for being too focused on miniatures and grids, too mechanical. Meanwhile, the company that owns <i>D&amp;D</i> had released a bunch of its old material for free as a service to fans, and some of that was built up into a competing game called <i>Pathfinder</i>. <i>Pathfinder</i> ultimately became more popular, by some metrics, than <i>D&amp;D</i> itself". Bohn highlighted that the 5th Edition was "designed for one purpose: to bring <i>D&amp;D</i> back to its roots and win back everybody who left during the edition wars".<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Henry Glasheen, for <i><a href="/wiki/SLUG_Magazine" title="SLUG Magazine">SLUG Magazine</a></i>, highlighted that after jumping ship during the 4th Edition era he was drawn back to <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> with 5th Edition<sup id="cite_ref-:11_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> and he considers it "the new gold standard for D20-based tabletop RPGs".<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Glasheen wrote "Fifth Edition is a compelling reason to get excited about D&amp;D again" and "while some will welcome the simplicity, I fully expect that plenty of people will stick to whatever system suits them best. However, this edition is easily my favorite, ranking even higher than D&amp;D 3.5, my first love in D&amp;D".<sup id="cite_ref-:11_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Curtis D. Carbonell, in the book <i>Dread Trident: Tabletop Role-Playing Games and the Modern Fantastic</i>, wrote: "Negative association with earlier niche 'nerd' culture have reversed. 5e has become inclusive in its reach of players, after years of focusing on a white, male demographic. [...] At its simplest, the game system now encourages different types of persons to form a party not just to combat evil [...] but to engage in any number of adventure scenarios".<sup id="cite_ref-:02_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>82&#8211;83</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acclaim">Acclaim</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Acclaim">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The various editions of <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> have won many <a href="/wiki/Origins_Award" title="Origins Award">Origins Awards</a>, including <i><a href="/wiki/1977_Origins_Award_winners" title="1977 Origins Award winners">All Time Best Roleplaying Rules of 1977</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/1989_Origins_Award_winners" title="1989 Origins Award winners">Best Roleplaying Rules of 1989</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/2000_Origins_Award_winners" title="2000 Origins Award winners">Best Roleplaying Game of 2000</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/2014_Origins_Award_winners" title="2014 Origins Award winners">Best Role Playing Game and Best Role Playing Supplement of 2014</a></i> for the flagship editions of the game.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Both <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> and <i>Advanced Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> are Origins Hall of Fame Games inductees as they were deemed sufficiently distinct to merit separate inclusion on different occasions.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The independent <i><a href="/wiki/Games_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Games (magazine)">Games</a></i> magazine placed <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> on their <i>Games 100</i> list from 1980 through 1983, then entered the game into the magazine's Hall of Fame in 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> <i>Advanced Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> was ranked 2nd in the 1996 reader poll of <i>Arcane</i> magazine to determine the 50 most popular roleplaying games of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-Arcane14_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arcane14-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/National_Toy_Hall_of_Fame" title="National Toy Hall of Fame">National Toy Hall of Fame</a> in 2016 and into the <a href="/wiki/Science_Fiction_and_Fantasy_Hall_of_Fame" class="mw-redirect" title="Science Fiction and Fantasy Hall of Fame">Science Fiction and Fantasy Hall of Fame</a> in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_influence">Legacy and influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dungeons_%26_Dragons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Legacy and influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> was the first modern role-playing game and it established many of the conventions that have dominated the genre.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Particularly notable are the use of dice as a <a href="/wiki/Game_mechanic" class="mw-redirect" title="Game mechanic">game mechanic</a>, character record sheets, use of <a href="/wiki/Statistic_(role-playing_games)" title="Statistic (role-playing games)">numerical attributes</a> and gamemaster-centered group dynamics.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Within months of <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons'</i>s release, new role-playing game writers and publishers began releasing their own role-playing games, with most of these being in the fantasy genre. Some of the earliest other role-playing games inspired by <i>D&amp;D</i> include <i><a href="/wiki/Tunnels_%26_Trolls" title="Tunnels &amp; Trolls">Tunnels &amp; Trolls</a></i> (1975),<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Empire_of_the_Petal_Throne" title="Empire of the Petal Throne">Empire of the Petal Throne</a></i> (1975), and <i><a href="/wiki/Chivalry_%26_Sorcery" title="Chivalry &amp; Sorcery">Chivalry &amp; Sorcery</a></i> (1976).<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The role-playing movement initiated by <i>D&amp;D</i> would lead to release of the science fiction game <i><a href="/wiki/Traveller_(roleplaying_game)" class="mw-redirect" title="Traveller (roleplaying game)">Traveller</a></i> (1977), the fantasy game <i><a href="/wiki/RuneQuest" title="RuneQuest">RuneQuest</a></i> (1978), and subsequent game systems such as <a href="/wiki/Chaosium" title="Chaosium">Chaosium</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Call_of_Cthulhu_(role-playing_game)" title="Call of Cthulhu (role-playing game)">Call of Cthulhu</a></i> (1981), <i><a href="/wiki/Champions_(roleplaying_game)" class="mw-redirect" title="Champions (roleplaying game)">Champions</a></i> (1982), <i><a href="/wiki/GURPS" title="GURPS">GURPS</a></i> (1986),<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Vampire:_The_Masquerade" title="Vampire: The Masquerade">Vampire: The Masquerade</a></i> (1991).<sup id="cite_ref-Schick_17-34_70-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schick_17-34-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> and the games it influenced fed back into the genre's origin&#160;– miniatures wargames&#160;– with combat strategy games like <i><a href="/wiki/Warhammer_Fantasy_Battles" class="mw-redirect" title="Warhammer Fantasy Battles">Warhammer Fantasy Battles</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> <i>D&amp;D</i> also had a large impact on modern <a href="/wiki/Video_game" title="Video game">video games</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>D&amp;D</i> has been compared unfavorably to other role-playing games of its time. Writing for <a href="/wiki/Slate_(magazine)" title="Slate (magazine)">Slate</a>, Erik Sofge makes unfavorable comparisons between the violent incentives of <i>D&amp;D</i> and the more versatile role-playing experience of <i>GURPS</i>. He claims that "for decades, gamers have argued that since D&amp;D came first, its lame, morally repulsive experience system can be forgiven. But the damage is still being done: New generations of players are introduced to RPGs as little more than a collective fantasy of massacre."<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> This criticism generated backlash from <i>D&amp;D</i> fans. Writing for <a href="/wiki/Ars_Technica" title="Ars Technica">Ars Technica</a>, Ben Kuchera responded that Sofge had experienced a "small-minded <a href="/wiki/Dungeon_Master" title="Dungeon Master">Dungeon Master</a> who only wanted to kill things", and that better game experiences are possible.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Director <a href="/wiki/Jon_Favreau" title="Jon Favreau">Jon Favreau</a> credits <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> with giving him "...&#160;a really strong background in imagination, storytelling, understanding how to create tone and a sense of balance."<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
